<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

Our architecture is based on a mobile device with a WLAN receiver, a textile RFID tag, and one or several RFID readers communicating with the mobile. The proposed passive textile RFID tag provides a very cost effective, power efficient, and easily implemented solution for human positioning and tracking applications. In addition, the joint utilization of two technologies increases the accuracy of the indoor positioning service. Our main contribution comes from the innovative RFID-WLAN hybrid architecture based on received signal strengths and able to improve the localization accuracy compared to pure RFID and pure WLAN location solutions. The proposed algorithm is tested with real-field measurements.</div>

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>The Indoor localization with low complexity and high accuracy is one of the main challenges in today's wireless world. Most promising technologies to be used indoors are currently based on wireless local area network (WLAN) solutions <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>. Alternative solutions have been proposed based, for example, on infra-red light, ultra-wide-band (UWB) systems, Bluetooth, and RFID <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a>. It is well understood that a low complexity can be achieved when using easily accessible information, such as received signal strength (RSS) and many indoor localization solutions are based on RSS <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>. The main challenges remain in achieving the best possible accuracy with the available signals, and this is the part we investigate in here, assuming that the available signals are the WLAN and RFID signals. Indeed, the RFID technology has become increasingly popular over recent years for tracking and positioning applications in indoor environments, as a cost-effective and power efficient solution that can be installed easily on different objects or people.</p><p>The synergical use of WLAN and RFID positioning has been very scarcely addressed in the current literature so far. One example where this problem is tackled can be found in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>, the mobile device was assumed to be equipped with both a WLAN receiver and an RFID reader, and several passive RFID tags were placed inside a room. The RFID backscattered powers from the tags were only used as an indicator of the zone where the mobile was placed, and the position estimation was purely based on WLAN RSS fingerprinting, applied in the zone identified by the RFID tags. Pure RFID-based localization was not studied, and neither was it included in the comparisons provided in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>.</p><p>The results published in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_1">[11]</a> shows that the channel modeling based on the RFID measurements are very promising. Moreover, the standard deviation of shadowing even shows more stability of RFID measured data compared with the WLAN measurement. However, utilizing the pure RFID technology or pure WLAN has its own limitations <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>. In this work, we present a hybrid solution to increase the robustness and accuracy of indoor positioning systems, especially in harsh propagation conditions. On the one hand, using the textile RFID system provides high accuracyâwithin the limited distanceâand an easily implemented solution for tracking people in indoor scenarios. On the other hand, a very good coverage can be achieved utilizing the WLAN. The novelty of our letter is twofold: first, we propose a novel hybridization architecture based on RSS collected from RFID and WLAN systems; and second, we compare the performance of the hybrid approach with the pure WLAN and pure RFID localization solutions, based on measurement data performed in a university room.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 1. </b><fig><p>Textile passive UHF rfid tag.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7047740/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Measurement Setup</h2></div><p>To prepare the database for the fingerprinting, the RSS measurements were carried out in one university room in Tampere, utilizing the RFID system and WLAN system. The RFID experiment has been done using a wearable passive UHF RFID tag installed on the human arm. The measurement procedures are explained below in more detail.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec2a"><h3>A. Tag Fabrication</h3><p>The wearable tag is easy to be integrated with clothing due to its flexible conductive materials (electro-textile). Thus, it is a great solution for monitoring human movement and tracking. In addition, it can provide a reliable and efficient wireless communication link between body-worn electronics and the surrounding environment. The utilized sample for the measurement is a simple dipole UHF passive RFID tag fabricated by electro-textile. As shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 1</a>, the dipole is a copper fabric that is applied on top of a polyimide-based thin substrate. The commercial NXP G2iL chip is glued to the fabric using conductive epoxy. The tag has been designed for the frequency 850â960Â MHz, and the read range of the tag in free air measurement is about 8 m. More detail about the electrical properties of the copper fabric and the tag design is available on <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_2a">[10]</a>.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b"><h3>B. Data Collection Using RFID Tag</h3><p>The RSS measurements were performed in an indoor area. We made the measurement grids on the floor based on the schematic illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a> (left). width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-2">0.5~\hbox{m}\times 0.5~\hbox{m}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>) including total 169 measurement points were marked on the floor [<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a> (right)]. To have a better understanding of measured environment, the origin coordinate and few measurement points, matched with the schematic, are marked in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a> (right photos).
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 2. </b><fig><p>The left picture shows the schematic of the measurement region (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-23" role="math" style="width: 5.162em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 4.104em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.392em 1004.1em 2.45em -999.997em); top: -2.245em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-24"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-25"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-26"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-27" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">6Â m</span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-28" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.202em;">Ã</span><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-29" style="padding-left: 0.202em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-30"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-31" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">6Â m</span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.252em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.079em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.004em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-3">\hbox{6~m}\times \hbox{6~m}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> area inside a room). The deployed experiments are shown in the right pictures. The star spots show examples of the measurement points matched with the left picture.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7047740/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>To have good coverage in the room, four reader antennas were placed in each center edge of the square area. The antenna placement was done following an optimization process, while trying to maximize the overall coverage of all readers. All four antennas are vertical polarized from <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-4-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-32" role="math" style="width: 7.811em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 7.03em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1007.03em 2.465em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-33"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-34"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-35"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-36"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-37"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-38" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Huber</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-39" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">+</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-40" style="padding-left: 0.243em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-41"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-42"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-43"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-44" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Suhner</span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.13em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.003em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-4">{\hbox{Huber}}+{\hbox{Suhner}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (SPA 920/65/10) with the gain of 9.5Â dBi. The antennas are connected to an RFID reader that is placed in the center of the square (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 2</a>). The reader is an Impinj Speedway R420, including four antenna ports, which is capable of communicating with all four antennas simultaneously. The reader is adjusted to the ETSI frequency. In addition, the reader is connected to the computer through the LAN connection for storing the scanned data. The maximum transmitted power of the Impinj reader is about 30 dBm. To not exceed the equivalent isotropically radiated power (EIRP), the transmitted power for the measurement is adjusted to the 28 dBm with respect to the loss of the cables and gain of the antennas. The copper fabric tag has been installed on the human arm vertically to keep the same polarization as the reader antennas.</p><p>However, for a practical implementation of our proposed hybrid solution, we would need a WLAN connection from the reader to the mobile device. In here, we prove that an RFIDâWLAN localization solution with wearable tags is fully feasible, but we do not offer the full commercial architecture. The recommended architecture is shown later in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 5(b)</a>.</p><p>The backscattered power of the tag is measured by moving the person between all 169 measurement positions. For the first scan, the tag was facing one specific antenna, and the RSS values for 169 measurement points were measured. The same scenario has been repeated for the second and third scan, and the time interval between each scan was about 2 h. This measurement has been repeated four times, with the tag facing one specific antenna each time to be able to compare the results with similar orientations in the training and estimation phases and with different antenna orientations in these phases (the latter case being the most likely one in a commercial implementation). The same type of measurements in 42 random coordinates is also conducted for the estimation phase. This time only one scan per measurement points is performed; however, it is repeated for the tag facing four reader antennas. The block diagram of the pure RFID location solution is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 3</a> (block 1).
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig.Â 3. </b><fig><p>Block 1: pure rfid/pure WLAN location. Block 2: proposed hybrid RFID-wlan positioning algorithm.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7047740/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2c"><h3>C. WLAN Data Collection</h3><p>The data collection for the WLAN was done similarly with <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_2c">[2]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_2c">[3]</a>. In the same measurement room as above, the WLAN data have been collected utilizing a Nexus tablet with HERE maps. The tablet had software capable of reading the destination map and converting it to the Cartesian coordinates. During the data collection, the position on the map is manually added, and the RSS values from multiple access points (APs) at that point are read. The measurements were taken at the same coordinates as for RFID to enable the hybridization. The pure WLAN positioning solution shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">FigÂ 3</a> (block 1) is based on traditional fingerprinting with Gaussian likelihood function <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_2c">[2]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_2c">[5]</a>.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Proposed Hybridization Algorithm</h2></div><p>Our proposed hybridization architecture is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 3</a> (block 2). It has two stages: a training stage shown in the upper part, and an estimation phase, where the actual hybridization is done. In the training (offline) phase, received signal strengths from WLAN AP or backscattered powers from RFID readers are collected and stored in (<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-5-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-45" role="math" style="width: 7.871em; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.784em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-252" style="font-size: 70.7%; Our convention is that the last <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-20-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-255" role="math" style="width: 1.444em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.264em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1001.26em 2.465em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-256"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-257"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-258"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-259"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.264em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.9em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-260" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">N<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.784em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-261" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">2</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.197em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-20">{N_2}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> RSS values belong to the RFID signals so that we can tackle jointly the WLAN and RFID measurements. In addition, if an AP is not heard in a certain point, the convention is that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-21-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-262" role="math" style="width: 2.465em; <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ I</a> illustrates the characteristics of the measured environment in terms of the mean and maximum ranges at which a transmitter is heard; the minimum, maximum, and average number of heard transmitters in each measured point inside the room; and the estimated shadowing standard deviation, based on the training data. <div class="figure figure-full table" id="table2"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TableÂ II </b>Percentage of estimated points with accuracy below 2Â m<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-table-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/7024204/7047740/7047740-table-2-small.gif" alt="Table " /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-46-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1137" role="math" style="width: 0.604em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.544em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.54em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1138"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-1139" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">a</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-46">a</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is an optimization parameter, which selects the threshold between using RFID or WLAN-based estimates. The optimization of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-47-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1140" role="math" style="width: 0.604em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.544em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.54em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1141"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-1142" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">a</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-47">a</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 4</a>, based on the distance root mean square error (RMSE) as the optimization criterion. The optimization of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-48-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1143" role="math" style="width: 0.604em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.544em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.54em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1144"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-1145" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">a</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-48">a</script></tex-math></inline-formula> was done with a different set of estimation data than that one used in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 5</a> and <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ II</a>. It was found that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-49-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1146" role="math" style="width: 4.327em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.907em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1003.85em 2.405em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1147"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-1148" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">a</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-1149" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-1150" style="padding-left: 0.303em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1151"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-1152" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">2Â dB</span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-49">a = \hbox{2~dB}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> gives the best results, meaning that we trust the RFID positioning as long as the RFID signal strength is higher than the WLAN signal strength with a 2-dB margin.<p></p><p>The results of the hybridization algorithm (<a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 3</a>), compared with pure WLAN and pure RFID positioning, are shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 5</a> and in terms of the cumulative distribution function of the distance error and of the percentage of estimated track points with a distance error below 2Â m, respectively. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 5</a>, we show what happens when only <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-50-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1153" role="math" style="width: 0.964em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.844em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.84em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1154"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-1155" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">N<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-50">N</script></tex-math></inline-formula> out of the four RFID readers are used, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-51-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1156" role="math" style="width: 5.649em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 5.048em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1005.05em 2.586em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1157"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-1158"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-1159"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-1160" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">N<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-1161" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-1162" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">1</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-1163" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">,</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-1164" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.183em;">â¦</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-1165" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.183em;">4</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-51">{N}=1,\ldots 4</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Clearly, the more RFID readers available, the better the accuracy of the location solution. The pure RFID solution is unable to offer a position estimate in 40% to 80% of the cases (depending on whether we use four or less RFID readers), and this is because the reader range is limited, and there are many points inside the room where no backscatter power reaches the readers. On the other hand, a pure WLAN positioning solution can always offer a position estimate. Pure RFID solutions are indeed slightly better at very low distance errors (lower than 1Â m), but this kind of small errors are achieved with a low probability. <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">TableÂ II</a> illustrates the probabilities (given as percentages) of achieving a distance error less than 2Â m with any of the considered algorithms. Hyrid RFIDâWLAN solutions are clearly the best, even with only one RFID reader. The results clearly show the benefits of a hybrid RFIDâWLAN architecture, benefits that can be incorporated in a commercial architecture, as illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">FigÂ 5(b)</a>.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>In this letter, we have introduced a novel hybrid WLANâRFID indoor localization solution. The hybrid solution is suitable for the indoor positioning in environments where both RFID and WLAN signals are available, since it takes advantage of both types signals. The textile passive RFID tag proposes very cost-effective, easily implemented, and efficient configuration for human tracking in harsh environments, and it can be deployed for other purpose such as access control or patient tracking in health care applications. The presented configuration based on the hybrid model can be expanded for larger indoor area utilizing more RFID tag and readers, as illustrated in our design recommendation in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig.Â 5(b)</a>.</p><p>Our experimental results show that our hybrid solution can enhance the probability to get errors below 2 m with several percents even if only one RFID reader is available and that RSS coming from two different systems can be successfully combined towards a joint hybridization solution.</p></div>
</div></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>
